No instances exist yet, so there is nothing to cancel and no execution-context hazard in planning. (3) INSTANTIATION: identical to configuration-classes — async Factories under per-bean guards, boxed-future at the dyn edge, fail-fast eager singletons. Diagnostics fire at the COLD pass: an enabled feature with zero contributors, an `Enable<A>` whose attribute fails validation, an ordering cycle, or a `Registrar` violating the post-processor rule all surface before any async work, richly attributed. Because there is no deferred-selector reentrancy and no Group merge, the planning phase is the simplest of the three realizations: gather, condition, order, register — then construct.

#### R3 — Build-time IMPORT-GRAPH compiler: a codegen pass walks `@Import` edges, runs selectors/groups AT BUILD TIME, and emits one frozen, ordered, conditioned assembly plan; `@Enable*` attributes inlined
Question whether ANY of the import composition needs to happen at runtime. Spring resolves the import graph at runtime because the JVM discovers config reflectively; leaf has a codegen seam (charter §3, Arch C / sqlx model). So make `@Import` a BUILD-TIME GRAPH COMPILER.

A leaf-codegen pass (build.rs or a `cargo leaf prepare` step) over the resolved dependency graph + a manifest of contributors does, at BUILD time, what Spring does at runtime: (a) walk altitude-1 `#[import]` edges transitively, de-dup the active config set; (b) EXECUTE compile-time-evaluable selectors (selectors expressed as const/declarative rules, or as build-time functions the codegen invokes) to expand the candidate set; (c) run the GROUP merge — de-dup + `@Order`/before/after topo-sort with cycle detection — over the whole batch; (d) evaluate the build-time-decidable CONDITIONS (`OnClass`-analogues = is the crate/feature in the resolved graph?, profiles known at build) to PRUNE; (e) emit a single frozen, ordered `assembly_plan.rs` `include!`d into the binary: an explicit ordered list of definition contributions + their conditions that remain RUNTIME-decidable (e.g. `OnMissingBean`, which needs the live registry). `@Enable*` attributes are read at build time and INLINED into the plan (the importee's attribute values become const data in the generated plan — the strongest form of ImportAware: importer metadata frozen into generated code, exactly Spring's AOT `BeanRegistrarAotContribution` 're-invoke with reattached metadata' but native).

The imperative `Registrar` (altitude 4) is the one piece that may stay RUNTIME (instance-supplier re-invoked at startup, AOT-style), since arbitrary imperative registration can't always be frozen — matching Spring AOT's `aotProcessingIgnoreRegistration` re-invoke pattern.

The runtime then has NO import/selector/group machinery at all: it consumes a flat, ordered, mostly-pre-conditioned plan and only evaluates the residual live-registry conditions before constructing. This is the AOT-extreme: the entire `@Import` + DeferredImportSelector.Group + ordering pipeline that Spring runs at startup is moved to build time and frozen.

**Pros:** Most AOT-faithful and lowest startup overhead (§2.1 #2): the whole import-resolution + group-merge + ordering pipeline runs at BUILD time and is frozen into a flat ordered plan — startup just reads it and evaluates residual live conditions; best fit for the compile-vs-runtime split leaning build-time, and the deterministic/portable Arch C substrate (works on wasm/embedded where link-time slices fail).; Earliest-richest diagnostics (§2.9): an unsatisfiable import edge, an import/auto-config ordering CYCLE, an excluded class that isn't actually an auto-config (Spring's `checkExcludedClasses` typo-guard), or a selector producing a duplicate all fail at the BUILD/plan step — strictly earlier than R1/R2's startup pass, with the generated plan file inspectable (sqlx-style checked-in, `--check`-able artifact for CI).; Strongest ImportAware realization: importer `@Enable*` attributes are frozen into generated code as const data — no runtime metadata walk at all, the truest 'no special enable machinery' (the attributes literally ARE the generated wiring), and the native analogue of Spring's AOT metadata reattachment.; The generated `assembly_plan.rs` is a single, ordered, human-readable manifest of the ENTIRE composed configuration — exceptional debuggability and the natural place for a `--check` that catches silently-lost contributions (the anti-DCE self-check becomes a build-time plan diff, not a runtime count).; Eliminates the deferred-batch lifecycle phase from RUNTIME entirely — deferral-for-correctness is honored by build-time ordering + the residual-condition split, so the runtime never runs a selector/group at all.

**Cons:** PRE-DECIDES the metadata/codegen boundary AND the compile-vs-runtime split — both explicit Phase-2 keystones (§3); presenting build-time import-graph compilation as THE shape biases that signal hard, so it must be held strictly as a CANDIDATE, the most coupled-to-an-undecided-meta-architecture of the three.; CROSS-CRATE is the killer: a build.rs/proc-macro sees only its OWN crate (T1 hard wall), so a codegen pass in the BINARY can only resolve the import graph from a MANIFEST of declared contributors (cargo-metadata-driven), not by truly walking foreign crates' `@Import` annotations — a config in crate A importing crate B is fine (A references B), but a deferred selector contributed by crate C whose decision depends on crate D's runtime state cannot be frozen; such cases must degrade to a RUNTIME residual, reintroducing R1/R2's runtime pass for exactly the dynamic cases selectors exist for.; Build-time selector execution is only as powerful as what's evaluable at build time: a selector that decides from a runtime property value, environment, or live bean presence CANNOT run at build time and must stay runtime — so 'run selectors at build time' is PARTIAL, and the split between build-time-frozen and runtime-residual selectors/conditions is a new authoring-rule surface and a potential silent-divergence source.; Heaviest machinery of the three (an import-graph compiler, group-merge, topo-sort, condition-pruner, plan emitter, `--check`) — in tension with thin-macros (§2.10) UNLESS all heavy logic lives in a normal leaf-codegen crate the macro merely feeds (which the charter allows), but it is still the most to specify, test, and keep idempotent (OUT_DIR hygiene, deterministic codegen).; WASM-module dynamic source has NO build-time presence (its configs/edges aren't in the resolved cargo graph), so any import involving a dynamically-loaded module can ONLY be a runtime residual — the frozen-plan shape and the dynamic-directory 'classpath' are in direct tension here, a flagged Phase-2 question.

**Mechanisms considered:** build-time IMPORT-GRAPH compiler (leaf-codegen / cargo leaf prepare): walk `#[import]` edges, run build-evaluable selectors, group-merge + topo-sort, prune build-decidable conditions, emit frozen ordered `assembly_plan.rs`; split conditions into BUILD-decidable (crate/feature-in-graph = OnClass-analogue, known profiles) pruned at build vs RUNTIME-residual (OnMissingBean over live registry) evaluated at startup; `@Enable*` importer attributes read at build time and INLINED as const data into the generated plan (strongest ImportAware; native analogue of Spring AOT metadata reattachment); imperative `Registrar` stays RUNTIME (instance-supplier re-invoked at startup, AOT `aotProcessingIgnoreRegistration` re-invoke pattern) — the one piece not frozen; cargo-metadata/manifest-driven contributor enumeration (the binary's codegen can't see foreign `@Import` annotations directly; it reads a declared manifest) — sqlx-style checked-in, `--check`-able plan artifact; runtime residual path for build-undecidable selectors/conditions and for any WASM-module dynamic-source imports — a fallback that reintroduces a (smaller) runtime pass; CANDIDATE not a decision

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Strongest fit for the build-time-codegen substrate (Arch C) and the only realization that works cleanly on wasm/embedded (no link-time slices). The codegen walks the resolved dependency graph + a contributor manifest and emits an explicit, force-linked, ordered plan — sidestepping ALL three DCE layers for the planned contributions (the plan literally names every contributor, so they are referenced). BUT the central T1 LIMITATION is severe and structural: a build.rs/proc-macro sees ONLY its own crate (the hard cross-crate wall), so the binary's codegen cannot introspect foreign crates' `@Import`/selector annotations — it must rely on each contributor crate emitting a build-time MANIFEST (or const descriptor) that the binary's pass aggregates via cargo metadata. Intra-crate and explicit name-referenced cross-crate edges (A imports B's named config) freeze cleanly; selectors/groups whose decision spans crates or depends on runtime state CANNOT freeze and degrade to a runtime residual (R1/R2-like), splitting the model into build-frozen vs runtime-resolved — the same intra-vs-cross-crate asymmetry that haunts the configuration-classes R3, here applied to the import graph. leaf-core hosts the plan/descriptor ABI; leaf-codegen owns the compiler; the binary runs plan-validation + the anti-DCE check as a BUILD diff (a missing manifest entry = loud build error, the best possible self-check). Generics can't register (hard-error). WASM-module dynamic source has no build-time presence -> its imports are runtime-residual only — flagged tension, CANDIDATE.

**T2 — execution context:** Inverts the phase split toward build time. The IMPORT-GRAPH COMPILATION (edge walk, build-evaluable selector execution, group merge, topo-sort, build-condition pruning, plan emission) is a SYNCHRONOUS BUILD-TIME pass — runs in build.rs / a CLI, single-threaded, NOT in the application's async runtime; it may do build-time IO (read manifests, cargo metadata) but produces only data (the generated plan). At STARTUP the runtime does almost nothing import-related: it reads the frozen ordered plan and evaluates ONLY the residual live-registry conditions (`OnMissingBean`-analogues) before constructing — cold, single-threaded, fast. The imperative `Registrar` residual runs at startup (synchronous definition minting, no IO/block). INSTANTIATION is the usual async path (per-bean guards, boxed-future at the dyn edge). Errors split by TIMING: build-decidable failures (unsatisfiable edge, ordering cycle, bad exclusion, duplicate) fail at the BUILD pass — earliest, richest, with the generated plan inspectable and a `--check` for CI; only residual-condition failures (a feature backs off unexpectedly) surface at startup. Cancellation is irrelevant to the build pass (it's offline) and only concerns phase-3 construction. The async/execution-context discipline (§2.3) barely touches this feature in R3 because all composition logic is build-time data production; the only async is downstream construction, unchanged from configuration-classes. The risk is the build-frozen/runtime-residual SEAM: a selector the author expects to run is silently build-frozen (or silently deferred to runtime), so the timing of WHERE a given import decision is made must be legible in the generated plan (it is, by inspection) — turning Spring's invisible runtime selector execution into a visible build artifact.

### Rust clashes
- NO RUNTIME CLASSPATH, so 'import a class to register its definitions' largely COLLAPSES INTO link-time/build-time contribution. Spring's altitude-1 `@Import(OtherConfig.class)` works because the JVM has a classpath and loads/parses the named class reflectively at runtime. In Rust, every config in every linked crate is ALREADY a contributor to the one descriptor stream (configuration-classes / component-scanning) — so 'importing a config to register its beans' is mostly what contribution already does. The realizations diverge on whether `@Import` survives as a distinct construct: R1 keeps it as a typed EDGE over the stream (which usefully path-references the importee, solving Layer-0 DCE for that edge); R2 dissolves it into plain contribution + the enable!/scan! crate list (the honest `@ComponentScan basePackages` analogue); R3 makes it a build-time graph compiler. None can faithfully reproduce a runtime classpath, because Rust has none.
- `selectImports` RETURNS CLASS-NAME STRINGS resolved against the classloader — NO RUST ANALOGUE. Spring's `ImportSelector.selectImports` returns `String[]` of fully-qualified class names that the classloader resolves at runtime — pure reflective, late binding. Rust cannot resolve a string to a type at runtime (no runtime reflection, no classloader). So a selector's output must be a CLOSED SET of already-linked types/markers (R1's `ImportRef`), or the 'pick which configs' decision must be reframed as a CONDITION over a pre-contributed candidate set (R2), or executed at BUILD time against the resolved graph (R3). The open, runtime-string-driven dynamism of `selectImports` (e.g. a selector returning a class name computed from a property) has no direct realization and must degrade to conditions/registrars over a known set.
- `ImportAware.setImportMetadata(AnnotationMetadata)` IS REFLECTIVE ATTRIBUTE READING — replace with a TYPED importer-attribute struct. Spring's `@Enable*` backbone works by the imported config reflectively reading the importer's annotation attributes off `AnnotationMetadata` at runtime (untyped, string-keyed). leaf has no runtime annotation reflection; the rust-native form is a TYPED attribute struct (`EnableConfig<A>` / `Enable<A>`) the macro emits from the `@Enable*` annotation's fields, threaded to the importee as injectable data — compile-checked, refactor-safe, and (R3) inlinable as const data. This is a strict DX/diagnostics IMPROVEMENT over Spring's stringly metadata, and it is the load-bearing realization of '@Enable* is sugar over @Import + metadata introspection' without any reflective 'enable machinery'.
- DEFERRAL FOR `@Conditional` CORRECTNESS depends on a definite 'all user config is registered' MOMENT — which is clean in Rust but couples to the (undecided) conditional strategy. Spring's DeferredImportSelector defers PRECISELY so `@ConditionalOnMissingBean` on auto-config sees the final user-definition set and backs off (a correctness mechanism, not a perf tweak). leaf can reproduce this as a definite runtime BATCH/CONDITIONAL phase after eager assembly (R1/R2) or as a build-time ordering + runtime-residual split (R3). But WHEN conditions are evaluated relative to definition assembly is partly the Phase-2 conditional-strategy question (cfg vs codegen vs runtime gate) — if conditions are partly compile-time, preserving the 'back off after seeing all user beans' guarantee needs care. The deferral intent transfers; the exact mechanism is entangled with an undecided keystone.
- `DeferredImportSelector.Group` GLOBAL MERGING is cross-crate ordering — and ORDERING MUST BE DATA, never link/section/constructor order. Spring's Group collects candidate imports across every selector sharing a group and returns ONE merged/de-duped/`@Order`-sorted batch — the engine assembling dozens of jars' auto-configs into a coherent batch. In Rust, the contributors are collected via link-time slices (unspecified, possibly randomized order) or codegen, so the merge/ordering MUST be computed from ORDERING-AS-DATA on each descriptor (before/after/priority) via a topological sort with cycle detection — never relying on collection order. This is exactly auto-config-ordering's job, so Group-merging unifies with it; the clash is only that leaf must NEVER lean on registration order the way a naive reading of 'process selectors in order' might suggest.
- SELECTORS/REGISTRARS AS RUNTIME OBJECTS run in an async-first world but are PLANNING-PHASE, sync-only logic. Spring's selectors/registrars are plain objects invoked synchronously during config parsing. In leaf they are descriptors collected cross-crate; the clash is execution-context discipline (§2.3): a selector/registrar runs in the COLD planning phase, single-threaded, BEFORE any instance exists — it must be PURE/synchronous (observe plan state + Environment, vote/mint definitions), MUST NOT block, do IO, or be async (mirroring the read-mostly ConditionContext discipline). A selector that 'needs to await something' is a design smell with no clean home — that work belongs to a bean's async Factory, not to import planning. The `*Aware`-vs-single-constructor dual handle-access of Spring collapses to ordinary parameter/handle injection on the (synchronous) selector/registrar.
- 'REGISTRAR CANNOT REGISTER A BDRPP' (a JVM lifecycle-ordering rule) maps to a leaf PLANNING-PHASE constraint that can be a LOUD COMPILE/PLAN ERROR. Spring forbids an ImportBeanDefinitionRegistrar from registering a BeanDefinitionRegistryPostProcessor because registrars run AFTER the BDRPP phase (silently unsupported). leaf has the same phase-ordering reality (definition-editing post-processors run in a fixed window per post-processor-spi / refresh-lifecycle), but because leaf's macro/engine can SEE the registrar's contributions, it can turn Spring's silent 'too late' into a loud diagnostic — a rich-diagnostics improvement (§2.9). Same flip as configuration-classes' lite-mode footgun-to-compile-error.
- GENERICS CANNOT REGISTER — a generic config/selector/registrar/group has no `'static` TypeId/symbol until instantiated. Spring resolves open-generic configs via runtime ResolvableType; leaf's link-time/codegen collection needs a concrete monomorphized value with a fixed symbol. So the macro must HARD-ERROR on a generic `#[configuration]`/selector/registrar with a `register_import!(ConcreteType)` per-instantiation shim — the same constraint that hits every contributor kind, applied to the import family.
- CROSS-CRATE IMPORT-GRAPH RESOLUTION can't be fully STATIC (R3-critical, general): a proc-macro/build.rs sees only its own crate. Spring's runtime classpath makes all `@Import` references uniform regardless of which jar they cross. In leaf, an altitude-1 import naming crate B's config from crate A is statically resolvable (A references B's type), but a selector/group whose decision spans crates or depends on a crate's runtime state cannot be resolved by either crate's build pass — it must degrade to a runtime residual over the whole-binary descriptor stream. So leaf's import graph splits into intra-crate / name-referenced (statically resolvable) vs span-crate/runtime-decided (runtime-resolved) — an asymmetry Spring's classpath hides and the AOT-extreme realization (R3) must live with.

### Interactions
- **configuration-classes** — The marquee SIBLING in the same area and the UNIT being composed: configuration-classes provides the `#[bean]`-bearing config + its typed Factory seeds; import-composition arranges, orders, defers, and gates those units at four altitudes. They are designed together — altitude-1 `@Import` pulls one config into another's active set, and `@Enable*`/ImportAware reads the IMPORTER config's typed attributes (`EnableConfig<A>`) into the imported config. Both emit thin descriptors over the SAME stream; an imported config's `#[bean]` definitions flow into the assembly exactly like any contributor's. R3's build-time import-graph compiler is the import-level counterpart of configuration-classes' R3 build-time bean lowering.
- **auto-configuration** — THE headline consumer. Spring's `AutoConfigurationImportSelector` IS a `DeferredImportSelector` whose `Group` merges every jar's auto-configs into one ordered, de-duped, conditionally-filtered batch that runs AFTER user config so `@ConditionalOnMissingBean` backs off. leaf's auto-configuration is built directly on this feature's deferred/group/ordering machinery (R1's batch phase, R2's conditional-pass-as-deferral, or R3's build-time merge). The 'opinionated but overridable' soul depends on the deferral-for-conditional-correctness intent realized here. Auto-config classes are lite-only configs (per configuration-classes) contributed and gated — import-composition is how they are assembled into a coherent whole.
- **auto-config-ordering** — Directly realizes `DeferredImportSelector.Group`'s merge/sort. Spring's `AutoConfigurationSorter` (alphabetical base -> `@AutoConfigureOrder` -> topological `@AutoConfigureBefore`/`After` with cycle detection) IS the Group's `selectImports` ordering. In leaf this MUST be ordering-as-DATA + a topo-sort with rich cycle diagnostics (never link/collection order). The Group-merge in R1, the conditional-pass ordering in R2, and the build-time merge in R3 all delegate to auto-config-ordering's topo-sort — they are the same machinery viewed from the import altitude vs the batch-ordering altitude. Note: ordering affects DEFINITION order (for condition correctness), not instantiation order (which is dependency-driven).
- **conditions** — Deeply entangled. In R2 the selector/deferred-selector altitudes DISSOLVE INTO conditions over a contributed candidate set; in R1/R3 conditions are evaluated within the deferred batch / build-time prune to honor the back-off-after-user-config guarantee. The deferral-for-conditional-correctness intent means import-composition's batch phase is precisely WHERE `@Conditional` (and especially `@ConditionalOnMissingBean`) on imported/auto-config definitions must be evaluated against the complete user-definition set. The two-phase parse-vs-register condition model (PARSE_CONFIGURATION vs REGISTER_BEAN) maps onto leaf's eager-vs-deferred altitude split.
- **condition-family** — The rich parameterized conditions (`OnClass`/`OnMissingClass`/`OnBean`/`OnMissingBean`/`OnProperty`) are what gate imported/auto-config candidates. In R3 the BUILD-decidable ones (OnClass-analogue = is the crate/feature in the resolved graph?) are evaluated at build time to prune the import batch, while runtime-residual ones (OnMissingBean over the live registry) stay runtime — the build-vs-runtime condition split is a core R3 mechanism. condition-family supplies the predicates; import-composition supplies the WHEN (deferred batch / build prune) they run.
- **programmatic-registration** — Altitude 4 (`ImportBeanDefinitionRegistrar`) UNIFIES with programmatic-registration. Spring 7's `BeanRegistrar` (functional `register(BeanRegistry, Environment)`, imported via `@Import`, instance-supplier + AOT-analyzable) is the modern successor that DEPRECATES `ImportBeanDefinitionRegistrar` — so leaf's one imperative import hook IS programmatic-registration's `Registrar`, reachable via the import family. R2 keeps exactly this single hook; R3 leaves it as the one RUNTIME residual (re-invoked at startup, AOT `aotProcessingIgnoreRegistration` style). The 'registrar can't register a definition-phase post-processor' rule is enforced here as a loud diagnostic.
- **annotation-model** — `@Enable*` and ImportAware rest ON the annotation model: an `@Enable` annotation is a meta-annotation that composes `@Import` + a typed attribute set. leaf's annotation model (composable, user-extensible, attribute-forwarding `@AliasFor`-analogue, metadata readable without loading) is what lets `@Enable*` be 'just sugar': the macro reads the `@Enable` annotation's fields into the typed `EnableConfig<A>`/`Enable<A>` struct. Spring's reflective `AnnotationMetadata` read becomes a compile-time typed projection — the rustClash where stringly metadata becomes a typed struct lives at this boundary.
- **post-processor-spi** — Spring's whole import family is driven by `ConfigurationClassPostProcessor`, a BDRPP at HIGHEST_PRECEDENCE that parses `@Configuration`/`@Bean`/`@Import`/`@ComponentScan` and ADDS derived definitions before any other definition-editing post-processor runs. leaf's import-resolution phase is the analogue: it must run in the definition-EDITING window (before instance-creating post-processors), and registrars (altitude 4) mint definitions in that window. The 'registrar runs after the BDRPP phase so it can't register a BDRPP' lifecycle constraint is a post-processor-spi ordering fact surfaced as a loud diagnostic.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Import composition slots a distinct PLANNING window into the fixed-order refresh template: load/contribute definitions -> resolve import edges -> run immediate selectors -> run the DEFERRED batch + group merge (R1) / the conditional+ordering pass (R2) -> register registrar definitions -> (then the usual BFPP/BPP/eager-instantiation steps). The deferred phase MUST sit after all eager user-config contribution but before instantiation — its position in the template is what gives the back-off-after-user-config correctness. R3 moves most of this window to BUILD time, leaving only a thin residual in the runtime template.
- **component-scanning** — Sibling discovery mechanism over the same stream. `@ComponentScan` (discover components in scoped packages) and `@Import` (compose named configs) are both contribution mechanisms; in R2 altitude-1 import collapses toward the same enable!/scan! crate-list discipline component-scanning uses (the honest `basePackages` analogue, scoped not global). A `@Configuration` is discovered by scanning as a candidate, then composes others via `@Import` — the two interlock. The cross-crate doc's 'scope discovery, don't make it global' guidance governs both.
- **environment** — Selectors and registrars receive the `Environment` (Spring passes it via `*Aware` or single-constructor injection; `BeanRegistrar.register(registry, env)` takes it explicitly) to make profile/property-driven import/registration decisions during planning. leaf threads `&Environment` to selectors/registrars as a read-only planning input (mirroring read-mostly ConditionContext). This is also how the auto-config kill-switch / exclusion property (`spring.boot.enableautoconfiguration`, `spring.autoconfigure.exclude`) reaches the import batch.
- **profiles** — `@Profile` is just a `@Conditional`, so profile-gated imports/auto-configs ride the same condition path as condition-family; the deferred batch evaluates profile conditions against the active profile set in the Environment. A `BeanRegistrar` can branch on `env.matchesProfiles(...)` to conditionally mint definitions (Spring's canonical example). Profiles are a build-time-or-runtime-decidable condition feeding the import prune (R3) or the runtime batch (R1/R2).
- **bean-definition** — Altitude 4 registrars mint `RawBeanDefinition`s by hand (the same metamodel keystone configuration-classes' `#[bean]` seeds produce) — import composition is, at the registrar altitude, a producer of definitions below the `#[bean]` level. Imported configs' definitions and registrar-minted definitions all land in the same definition set keyed/merged the same way. The 'declare the most concrete type so matching works' rule applies to registrar-minted definitions' produced types.
- **aware-callbacks** — Spring's selectors/registrars obtain container handles (Environment/BeanFactory/ClassLoader/ResourceLoader) via `*Aware` callbacks that fire BEFORE registerBeanDefinitions, OR via single-constructor injection. In leaf this dual mechanism collapses to ordinary parameter/handle injection on the (synchronous, planning-phase) selector/registrar — the planning context is passed explicitly, not via a callback protocol. The aware-callbacks feature governs how infrastructure handles reach planning-phase code; import-composition is a consumer that needs only read-mostly planning handles.
- **resource-loading** — Spring's `ImportCandidates`/`@AutoConfiguration.imports` and `@ImportAutoConfiguration`'s per-annotation `.imports` files are RESOURCE-driven discovery of importable configs (read via the ResourceLoader). leaf's analogue is the contributor manifest / the optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module 'classpath' (the second source in the two-source duality) — resource-loading abstracts access to that directory/manifest. The static link-time slice is the default 'classpath'; resource-loading backs the optional dynamic source. CANDIDATE, not a decision.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Do leaf's FOUR ALTITUDES survive as distinct constructs, or do most collapse into the unified descriptor stream? R1 keeps all four as thin metadata over one stream; R2 dissolves altitudes 1-3 into contribution + conditions + ordering and keeps only the imperative Registrar + a typed Enable toggle; R3 makes the whole graph a build-time compiler. Since Rust has no runtime classpath and 'import a class to register its beans' is largely what cross-crate contribution already does, WHICH altitudes are genuinely needed as separate surfaces (vs reframed as conditions/contribution) is the central per-feature divergence — and it cross-cuts the conditional strategy and the cross-crate composition model.
- Is the `selectImports`-returns-arbitrary-class-NAMES dynamism needed at all, and if so how is it realized without a classloader? Spring selectors return runtime-resolved STRINGS. leaf can only offer a CLOSED set of linked types (R1's ImportRef), conditions over a pre-contributed candidate set (R2), or build-time selector execution (R3). Whether any real leaf use case needs runtime-string-driven config selection — and what it degrades to if so — is unresolved and depends on the conditional strategy and whether dependency-based auto-configuration exists.
- How is the DEFERRAL-FOR-CONDITIONAL-CORRECTNESS moment realized, and does it survive a compile-time conditional strategy? The 'back off after ALL user config is registered' guarantee needs a definite 'user config complete' point. R1/R2 make it a runtime batch/conditional phase; R3 splits build-decidable vs runtime-residual conditions. This is entangled with the Phase-2 conditional strategy (cfg vs codegen vs runtime gate) and the compile-vs-runtime split — if conditions are partly compile-time, preserving the back-off guarantee for cross-crate auto-config is non-trivial.
- How much import-graph resolution happens at BUILD time vs runtime? R3 freezes edge-walk + group-merge + ordering + build-decidable conditions into a frozen plan (earliest diagnostics, lowest startup cost, wasm-friendly); R1/R2 resolve at a runtime planning phase. This pre-touches the metadata/codegen boundary AND the compile-vs-runtime split (both §3 keystones) and the diagnostic-timing call (§2.9) — it must be held as a candidate axis, not pre-decided here.
- The INTRA-crate vs CROSS-crate import-edge asymmetry: an altitude-1 import naming crate B's config from crate A is statically resolvable (A references B), but a selector/group spanning crates or depending on runtime state cannot be frozen by either crate's build pass and must degrade to a runtime residual. How is this asymmetry expressed without confusing the user model, and does it differ across the linkme / codegen / explicit substrates? Cross-cuts the cross-crate composition model and bean-definition's descriptor ABI.
- Typed `Enable<A>`/`EnableConfig<A>` importer-attribute resolution: when a config is imported by TWO importers with different `@Enable*` attribute values, whose attributes does the importee receive? Spring's `setImportMetadata` has a (subtle) answer per importing class; leaf's typed-struct model needs an explicit rule (per-import-edge attribute instance? error on conflict?). This is a new injection KIND (importer-metadata injection) distinct from ordinary bean injection — its resolution semantics are unresolved and cross-cut the annotation model and autowiring-resolution.
- The TWO-SOURCE duality for importable configs: the static link-time slice / build-codegen set (default 'classpath') PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source whose configs/edges have NO build-time presence. R1/R2 accommodate a dynamic source naturally (runtime contribution); R3's build-time frozen plan is in direct tension with it (dynamic imports can only be runtime residuals). How the dynamic source reconciles with any build-time import-graph freezing is unresolved — a CANDIDATE, not a decision, and origin-agnostic (a WASM-contributed config must compose indistinguishably from a native one).
- The import-family anti-DCE self-check: a registration-only contributor crate (a selector/registrar/config the binary never path-references) yields ZERO discovered contributions silently — the worst-case 'empty registry' symptom. What counts the EXPECTED import edges / selectors / group members / registrars per source crate, and how does the self-check surface a missing IMPORTEE (R1 edge target not in the active set) or a feature-enabled-but-zero-contributors case as an early rich diagnostic? Does the manifest differ across linkme vs codegen vs explicit substrates (R3 turns it into a build-time plan diff)? Cross-cuts the cross-crate composition model.
- Where do selectors/registrars sit in the execution-context model, and is a purely SYNCHRONOUS planning phase tenable? All three realizations require selectors/registrars to be sync, non-blocking, IO-free planning logic (observe + vote/mint, like read-mostly ConditionContext), deferring all async/IO to bean Factories. Is that discipline always sufficient, or does some real import decision genuinely need to await (e.g. probing a dynamic module directory)? If so, where does that async work live without violating §2.3? Cross-cuts the async execution & context-propagation model and resource-loading.

## @Conditional Condition SPI  `[conditions]`

Spring's `@Conditional`/`Condition` SPI reduces ANY registration decision to a pure predicate `matches(ConditionContext, AnnotatedTypeMetadata) -> bool`. The intent leaf must replicate (not the Java mechanism):
(1) PURE PREDICATE over two inputs only — read-mostly container/world state (ConditionContext: bean registry, environment/properties+profiles, resource loader, class/type availability) AND the metadata of the *triggering annotation* (so one reusable Condition serves many parameterized annotations, e.g. `@Profile` is literally `@Conditional(ProfileCondition)` reading `Profile.value`; this is what stops a combinatorial explosion of bespoke conditions and is the whole reason `condition-family`'s OnClass/OnBean/OnProperty/... can be thin shells).
(2) AND SEMANTICS — every condition stacked on an element must pass; boolean OR/AND/NOR is a *higher* construct (nested-condition helpers), the base operator is conjunction.
(3) TWO-PHASE PARSE-vs-REGISTER model — the linchpin. Cheap, monotonic, order-INSENSITIVE conditions (class/type presence) evaluate EARLY ("parse"), pruning whole config trees before anything else is examined. Order-SENSITIVE conditions that introspect what-has-been-defined-so-far (OnBean/OnMissingBean/OnSingleCandidate) MUST evaluate LATE ("register"), after the full set of definitions exists, else they answer against a half-built world. The phase is an author-declared, first-class contract; the most common authoring mistake is forgetting to declare REGISTER phase on a bean-introspecting condition.
(4) READ-WITHOUT-CONSTRUCTING — a condition decides whether a bean exists at all, so it must read the triggering annotation's attributes and probe the world WITHOUT instantiating the guarded bean (Spring reads ASM bytecode so `@ConditionalOnClass` against an absent optional dependency never throws). The Rust-relevant core of this is "decide before running the constructor," not Java's classloader nuance.
(5) UNSATISFIED = GRACEFUL NO-BEAN, never an error — a false condition silently drops the definition; the pain (if any) surfaces later as an unsatisfied-dependency error, and accountability comes from a separate report (`condition-report`), not exceptions. This is the engine of "opinionated defaults that back off to the user" (`auto-configuration`).
leaf owns the SPI shape (the predicate contract, the two-phase ordering, the read-mostly context, AND-composition + nested helpers, and the no-error-on-miss semantics). It does NOT own: the rich parameterized condition set (`condition-family`), the match/no-match accounting (`condition-report`), the metadata view it reads (`annotation-model`), profiles (`profiles`), or what state the world exposes (`environment`, `bean-registry`).

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Runtime predicate SPI over a const condition-descriptor table (Spring-faithful: data-carried conditions, runtime two-phase evaluation)
A condition is a runtime trait object evaluated by the container, exactly mirroring Spring's intent but rust-shaped. `trait Condition: Send + Sync { fn matches(&self, ctx: &ConditionContext<'_>, meta: &AnnotationMetadata) -> ConditionOutcome; fn phase(&self) -> Phase { Phase::Parse } }` where `ConditionOutcome` is `{ matched: bool, reason: ConditionMessage }` (a match/no-match plus a rich human string — so writing a good message makes `condition-report` populate for free, per Spring). `Phase` is `Parse | Register`. The triggering annotation contributes, via the `annotation-model` const descriptor, a `&'static [ConditionEntry]` — each entry pairs a `ConditionId` (which reusable Condition to invoke) with that annotation's own attribute slice (so `OnPropertyCondition` reads `name="...", having_value="..."` straight off the metadata). The leaf attribute macro is THIN: `#[conditional(on_property(name="x.enabled", match_if_missing=true))]` emits only the const `ConditionEntry { id: leaf::cond::ON_PROPERTY, attrs: &[...] }` appended to the element's metadata descriptor — no logic. The container's registration planner walks the collected descriptor table (the cross-crate substrate — origin-agnostic `&[Descriptor]`), and in a fixed two-pass order: PASS 1 evaluates every `Phase::Parse` condition (AND-fold; first false short-circuits, drops the definition, records the no-match reason); PASS 2, after all surviving definitions are known, evaluates `Phase::Register` conditions against the now-complete registry view. AND-semantics is the fold; nested OR/AND/NOR are themselves Conditions (`AnyOf(&[ConditionId])`, `AllOf`, `NoneOf`) that recursively evaluate member conditions, choosing their own phase — so boolean logic composes without special-casing. `Condition` impls are plain hand-writable types in a normal crate (`leaf-conditions`), registered by stable `ConditionId` so a const descriptor can name one without a vtable in `const` context. The Condition object itself is resolved from `ConditionId` at runtime via a small registry the `condition-family` crate populates.

**Pros:** Most faithful to Spring intent (#2 intent-parity): a pure predicate over (world, metadata), reusable Conditions parameterized by attributes, AND-fold, author-declared phase — `condition-family` becomes thin annotations exactly as in Spring.; Open and origin-agnostic: conditions evaluate at runtime over the collected descriptor table, so a definition contributed by a statically-linked crate OR a future dynamic/WASM-module source is gated identically; new condition kinds are just new `Condition` impls + `ConditionId`s, no codegen change (DX #1 for condition AUTHORS).; Two-phase ordering is data resolved at runtime (never link/section order — honoring the cross-crate doc's hard rule), so OnBean-style order-sensitivity is correct by construction and `auto-configuration` back-off (`OnMissingBean` after user config) works.; Thin macros (#10): the macro emits one const `ConditionEntry`; ALL predicate logic lives in normal testable crates; `cargo expand` shows a legible const.; Rich diagnostics fall out (#9): `ConditionOutcome` carries the reason, feeding `condition-report` with no extra author work; unsatisfied is a recorded no-match, never an error (matches Spring's graceful-no-bean).

**Cons:** Predicate runs at runtime on the cold startup path — a per-definition evaluation cost (bounded, but nonzero) vs a compile-time-pruned approach; conflicts mildly with low-overhead #2 if the descriptor table is large (auto-config has hundreds of candidates — needs the cheap-Parse-pass prune Spring also relies on).; `ConditionId`→`Condition` indirection needs a runtime resolution step (a registry the family crate fills); that registry is itself a cross-crate discovery surface, so it rides the SAME DCE-hazard substrate as components (must be self-checked).; 'Read without constructing' is only as strong as the metadata view: a condition can read attributes and probe the registry without building the guarded bean, but if a condition needs to probe a TYPE's presence, leaf has no ASM analogue — `OnClass`/`OnMissingClass` must be reframed (a linked type is always 'present'; the real signal is a cargo-feature/cfg, see R2) — so this realization alone cannot fully express OnClass.; Order-sensitive Register-phase conditions inherit Spring's teaching caveat: meaningful only where 'what's defined so far' is stable (auto-config-last); on arbitrary user definitions the answer is ill-defined — leaf must scope/ document this just as Spring restricts OnBean to auto-config.

**Mechanisms considered:** runtime `trait Condition` object + `ConditionOutcome{matched,reason}`; const `ConditionEntry{ConditionId, attrs}` emitted onto the annotation-model metadata descriptor by a thin attribute macro; linkme/inventory descriptor table feeding a runtime two-pass (Parse then Register) evaluator; `ConditionId`→`Condition` runtime registry populated by the condition-family crate; nested-condition Conditions (`AnyOf/AllOf/NoneOf`) for boolean composition, each choosing its phase; topo/phase ordering as DATA on the descriptor, never link order

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The `Condition` trait, `ConditionContext`, `Phase`, `ConditionOutcome`, and the `ConditionEntry`/`ConditionId` ABI live in the stable `leaf-core` kernel; emitted code hard-codes absolute paths to them (proc-macro hygiene). The condition ENTRIES travel as const data inside each element's metadata descriptor, which rides whatever cross-crate discovery substrate the framework picks (link-time `linkme` slice default + optional dynamic directory-scanned/WASM source) — conditions do not invent their own channel, they piggyback the component/definition descriptor table, keeping discovery scoped not implicitly global. TWO discovery surfaces exist and both inherit the DCE hazard + Layer-0 'crate must be referenced' rule: (a) the definition descriptors being gated, (b) the `ConditionId`→`Condition` implementation registry the family crate populates — a startup self-check must assert the expected built-in Conditions resolved (an empty condition registry would silently pass everything or fail everything). Generics: a `Condition` impl is a concrete `'static` type, fine; but an entry on a generic guarded item `Foo<T>` has no symbol — the macro must hard-error or demand per-instantiation registration, same limitation as component discovery. Ordering across crates is by declared `Phase` + dependency data, never section order.

**T2 — execution context:** Evaluation is strictly COLD-PATH: the registration planner runs the two passes during container bring-up (`refresh-lifecycle`), before any eager singleton is instantiated and before any bean future is spawned. `Condition::matches` is SYNCHRONOUS by contract (a pure read over already-materialized world state — registry snapshot, resolved environment) — it must NOT `.await` and must NOT block, because it runs inside the bootstrap task; any async work a condition would want (e.g. probing a remote) is disallowed by the SPI shape, mirroring Spring's read-mostly side-effect-free contract. Cancellation: the planner phase is a bounded cold step, not cancellable mid-fold. Errors: a condition that cannot decide returns a no-match with a reason (graceful), it does NOT panic; a genuine evaluation fault (e.g. malformed attribute) is aggregated into the startup validation diagnostic (#9) before wiring proceeds, never surfaced via async cancellation. The Register pass has a documented happens-before: it runs only after the Parse pass and after all definitions are collected, so OnBean-style reads see a stable world.

#### R2 — Statically-resolved conditions: cfg/feature gates + build/codegen-folded predicates (compile-time pruning, zero runtime cost)
Push the decision as early as the input allows, so most conditions become COMPILE-TIME facts and never reach the runtime planner. Two tiers. TIER A (cfg/feature): a `#[conditional(on_feature="redis")]` or `on_class`-analogue maps to a Cargo feature / `#[cfg(...)]` — the guarded definition's registration descriptor is literally `#[cfg(feature="redis")]`, so when the capability is absent the registration item does not exist in the compiled rlib at all (Spring's `@ConditionalOnClass`-against-absent-optional-dependency intent realized rust-natively: 'class present' becomes 'integration crate/feature compiled in'). This is the rust-native answer to 'read without loading' — the type is gated out of compilation, not merely not-constructed. TIER B (codegen-folded const predicate): conditions whose inputs are knowable at build time (a fixed property in a build-time config, a target cfg like `cfg(target_arch)` for an `OnCloudPlatform`/`OnThreading`-analogue) are folded by `leaf-codegen`/`build.rs` into a `const MATCHED: bool`, and the macro emits the descriptor inside `if const { MATCHED } { ...register... }` or omits it. The two-phase model degenerates helpfully: cfg/const conditions are inherently PARSE-phase (order-insensitive, decided before anything runs); anything that CANNOT be const (OnBean, runtime property overlays, profiles) is explicitly NOT expressible here and is delegated to R1's runtime layer — so R2 is a fast PATH, not a total solution. Authoring stays declarative; the macro chooses cfg-emit vs const-fold vs runtime-defer based on the condition kind's declared 'earliest decidable' tier.

**Pros:** Best possible overhead (#2): statically-pruned definitions cost nothing at runtime and aren't even in the binary (cfg tier) — directly matches Spring Boot's build-time `spring-autoconfigure-metadata` pre-filter that discards most candidates cheaply before the expensive parse.; Strongest 'read without loading' (#5/intent): cfg-gating physically removes the optional-dependency type from compilation, so naming an absent capability can never cause a build/link error — a cleaner guarantee than Spring's ASM trick, and it eliminates the DCE-on-an-unused-registration risk for gated-out items (they don't exist).; Earliest, richest diagnostics for the static tier (#9): a mis-spelled feature or a const-fold contradiction is a compile error with a span; `compile_error!` on an impossible `cfg(all(a, not(a)))`-style condition beats Spring's runtime no-match.; Thin macros (#10): emits only `#[cfg(...)]`-wrapped const descriptors or a `const`-guarded register call — all hand-writable; the tier-selection logic lives in `leaf-codegen`, a normal tested crate.; Plays directly into the cross-crate feature model: a leaf integration feature both compiles the registration AND is the condition — unifying 'which crates participate' with 'which conditions hold'.

**Cons:** Frozen at build time: cannot express anything decided by RUNTIME state — OnBean/OnMissingBean (order-sensitive, needs the live registry), profile activation (env-overlaid), a property set by a runtime config source — so it is INTRINSICALLY PARTIAL and must compose with R1; choosing R2 alone would amputate the order-sensitive half of Spring's two-phase model.; cfg-gating interacts with the cross-crate composition hazards: a feature gates COMPILATION but does not force the crate to be LINKED (Layer 0) — so 'condition true' must ALSO trigger the force-link of the integration crate, coupling this feature to the discovery substrate decision in a way R1 avoids.; Non-additive-feature footgun: modeling a condition as a Cargo feature risks mutually-exclusive/`matchIfMissing`-style semantics that features cannot express (features only OR on, never un-set) — backend CHOICE must stay a runtime/profile decision, so only genuinely additive capability-presence conditions fit this tier.; Two evaluation locations (compile-time const/cfg vs runtime planner) doubles the mental model and the places a 'why did/didn't this register' answer can come from — must be reconciled in one `condition-report` view to stay accountable.; build.rs/codegen can only see its own crate (no sibling visibility), so a build-time-folded condition cannot introspect what OTHER crates define — it is limited to local + target-cfg + build-config inputs.

**Mechanisms considered:** `#[cfg(feature=...)]`/`#[cfg(target_...)]`-wrapped const registration descriptors (Tier A); `leaf-codegen`/build.rs folding const-knowable predicates into `const MATCHED: bool` + inline `const {}` guard (Tier B); cargo capability features as the rust-native `OnClass`/`OnMissingClass` analogue (capability present = integration crate compiled); macro tier-selection: cfg-emit vs const-fold vs runtime-defer per condition kind's 'earliest decidable' class; `compile_error!` for statically-contradictory conditions; explicit delegation of all runtime-state conditions to R1 (this realization is a fast path, not total)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** This is the realization MOST entangled with the cross-crate/tooling reality (T1). Tier A leans on Cargo features + cfg: a feature gates whether a registration descriptor is compiled, but per the cross-crate doc this does NOT guarantee linkage — so a true static condition must be paired with the binary-level force-link reference (`use leaf_redis as _;`) that the discovery substrate emits, or the gated-in registration silently vanishes (empty-registry trap). Tier B's build.rs can see only its own crate and target cfgs (`CARGO_CFG_*`), never sibling/downstream identities, so build-folded conditions are limited to local + target + build-config inputs — it cannot answer 'is bean X defined in some other crate' (that is structurally a binary/runtime question → R1). The condition KIND→tier mapping must live in the kernel ABI so macros across the workspace agree on what is static vs deferred. wasm/embedded targets (no reliable link sections) push even more toward this static/codegen path (Architecture C in the cross-crate doc), making R2 the more portable substrate there. Resolver discipline (`resolver="3"`) matters: a sibling test/example enabling a leaf feature could flip a Tier-A condition on for a binary that never asked — a feature-unification correctness variable conditions inherit.

**T2 — execution context:** Almost entirely COMPILE-TIME / build-time: cfg decisions at compilation, const-folds in `leaf-codegen`/build.rs (host process), nothing async, nothing at runtime for the statically-decided set — the guarded definition is simply present or absent in the binary. The only runtime touchpoint is the residual handoff to R1 for conditions that could not be statically resolved, which run on the cold planner path as in R1. Errors are compile-time `compile_error!`/build-script `cargo::error` for contradictions or unknown features (#9, earliest possible), produced before any executor exists, so they never interact with async cancellation. No hot-path cost, no blocking, no cancellation surface for the static tier. The build.rs/codegen step is itself a cold one-shot with no async.

#### R3 — Typed predicate values + nested boolean combinators as a small expression algebra (composable, statically-typed conditions; hybrid-friendly)
Treat the condition not as an opaque `bool`-returning object but as a small, typed, composable EXPRESSION the container interprets — making AND/OR/NOR first-class and the two-phase split a property of the leaf nodes rather than an author footgun. A condition is a value: `enum CondExpr { Leaf(ConditionId, AttrSlice), All(&'static [CondExpr]), Any(&'static [CondExpr]), Not(&'static CondExpr) }` — a const-constructible tree emitted by the macro from declarative syntax like `#[conditional(all(on_property("x"), any(on_feature("a"), on_feature("b"))))]`. The container has one evaluator: `fn eval(expr: &CondExpr, ctx) -> ConditionOutcome` that recurses, so AND (the Spring default when you stack annotations) is just `All([...])` and boolean composition needs no AnyNestedCondition/AllNestedConditions boilerplate (a strict DX win over Spring's subclass-the-helper requirement). Each `Leaf` carries the same reusable-parameterized-Condition idea as R1, but the PHASE is computed as a property of the expression tree: `expr.phase() = max(leaf phases)` (a tree containing any Register-leaf is a Register expression; an all-Parse tree evaluates at parse) — so the author never declares a phase, the framework infers the earliest sound phase from the leaves' declared classes. Crucially, the leaf's evaluator is INDIFFERENT to whether a `Leaf`'s value came from a compile-time const-fold (R2), a cfg (already pruned so the node is absent), or a runtime probe (R1) — `ConditionId` can resolve to a static `const MATCHED` or a runtime `Condition`, so this realization is the natural SEAM between R1 and R2. Profiles slot in as `Leaf(ON_PROFILE, ...)` reading `environment.active_profiles()` — `@Profile` is just a preset expression, exactly Spring's 'profiles ARE conditions'.

**Pros:** Boolean composition is first-class and zero-ceremony (DX #1): `all/any/not` are syntax, not subclassed helper conditions — strictly better than Spring's AnyNestedCondition/AllNestedConditions/NoneNestedConditions boilerplate, while preserving the AND-default (stacked = All).; Phase-inference removes the #1 Spring authoring mistake (forgetting to declare REGISTER_BEAN): the framework derives the earliest-sound phase from leaf classes, so an OnBean nested anywhere automatically defers the whole expression to the register pass — turning a silent correctness bug into an automatic guarantee (#9 spirit: prevent the error rather than diagnose it late).; Acts as the unifying SEAM between static (R2) and runtime (R1) evaluation: a `Leaf`'s `ConditionId` resolves to either a const fact or a runtime predicate, so leaf can const-fold the cheap leaves and runtime-eval only the order-sensitive ones WITHIN ONE expression — the 'likely a blend' the charter foresees, expressed as one data structure.; Thin macros (#10): emits a const `CondExpr` tree; the evaluator and phase-inference live in a normal crate; `cargo expand` shows a legible nested const. Origin-agnostic: the tree is data, gating native or dynamic-source definitions identically.; One `eval` path means one place produces `ConditionOutcome` reasons → `condition-report` sees a single coherent account including the boolean structure ('matched All: [on-property ✓, any: [feature-a ✗, feature-b ✓]]'), richer than Spring's flat outcome list.

**Cons:** A const expression tree of `&'static [CondExpr]` nodes is more elaborate const data than R1's flat entry list; recursive const construction is fine on stable but the ABI (the `CondExpr` enum) becomes a load-bearing kernel type that emitted code hard-codes — must be ultra-stable and minimal, raising the cost of ever changing it.; Phase-inference (`max(leaf phases)`) is a convenience that can SURPRISE: an author who nests a Register-leaf inside what they thought was a cheap parse-time prune silently makes the whole guard late — better than Spring's wrong-answer bug, but the inferred phase must be surfaced in diagnostics/report or it trades one confusion for another.; The R1↔R2 seam is powerful but means a single expression can straddle compile-time and runtime evaluation, which complicates 'when exactly is this decided' reasoning and the report's provenance (some leaves decided at build, some at startup).; Evaluating a tree is marginally more runtime work than a flat AND-fold for the purely-runtime case (recursion, indirection) — negligible but not zero on the cold path; the win is only realized if the static leaves are actually const-folded.; Still cannot, by itself, solve 'read without loading' for the type-presence case any better than R1/R2 — `OnClass` still must be reframed as a cfg/feature leaf (delegated to R2's tier), so R3 is a composition layer, not an independent answer to that sub-intent.

**Mechanisms considered:** const-constructible `CondExpr` tree enum (Leaf/All/Any/Not) emitted by a thin macro from `all/any/not(...)` syntax; single recursive `eval(&CondExpr, ctx) -> ConditionOutcome` interpreter in a normal crate; framework-inferred phase = `max` over leaf-declared phase classes (no author phase declaration); `ConditionId` resolving to EITHER a const `MATCHED` fact (R2) or a runtime `Condition` (R1) — the static/runtime seam; `@Profile`/condition-family presets as canned `CondExpr` constructors; structured outcome tree feeding condition-report with boolean provenance

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same kernel residency as R1: `CondExpr`, `ConditionId`, `ConditionContext`, `ConditionOutcome`, `Phase` in `leaf-core` with absolute-path references from emitted const trees. The expression tree is const data riding the shared definition-descriptor discovery substrate (link-time slice default + optional dynamic/WASM source), origin-agnostic. Because a `Leaf` can resolve to a const-folded fact (R2) OR a runtime predicate (R1), this realization explicitly spans both the build-time and runtime cross-crate surfaces: cfg-gated leaves vanish from compilation (no descriptor) while runtime leaves ride the table — so the SAME element's guard may be partly resolved at compile time (in its own crate) and partly at the binary/runtime (where the full world is visible). The `ConditionId`→implementation/const resolution registry is the cross-crate hazard surface to self-check (an unresolved id must be a loud startup error, not a silent pass). Generics limitation identical: a guard on `Foo<T>` has no symbol; macro hard-errors or requires per-instantiation registration. Phase inference is pure runtime data computation over the tree — never link order.

**T2 — execution context:** Cold-path, like R1: the recursive `eval` runs in the registration planner during `refresh-lifecycle` bring-up, synchronously, no `.await`, no blocking — the whole tree is a pure read over already-materialized world state. Phase inference means a tree containing a Register-leaf is held back to the second pass automatically, preserving the happens-before that OnBean reads require (full definition set known before evaluation). Const-folded leaves (the R2 seam) are decided at compile/build time and are simply already-`true`/already-absent by the time the runtime evaluator sees the tree — so the runtime evaluator only recurses over the residual runtime leaves. Errors: a leaf returns a graceful no-match with a reason (never panics); a structural fault (unresolved `ConditionId`, malformed attrs) is aggregated into the startup validation diagnostic before wiring (#9). No cancellation surface on the read path; the planner phase is a bounded, non-cancellable cold step. Nothing here spawns a future or crosses a thread.

### Rust clashes
- No runtime reflection / no ASM-style 'read class without loading': Spring's signature trick — `@ConditionalOnClass` reads bytecode so naming an ABSENT optional dependency never throws — has NO direct Rust analogue. A linked type is always 'loaded'; you cannot name a type that isn't in the dependency graph. The rust-native realization of the *intent* (decide WITHOUT triggering the cost/failure of the optional dependency) is a CARGO FEATURE / cfg gate (capability present = integration crate compiled in), which physically removes the absent type from compilation. This reframes `OnClass`/`OnMissingClass` from a runtime metadata probe into a compile-time cfg fact — a different mechanism for the same intent, and the reason the conditional strategy is 'likely a blend' (charter §3).
- Spring's `Condition` is a runtime SPI evaluated by a reflective container that holds GC'd shared state; Rust has no reflection and ownership/borrowing constrains what 'read-mostly container state' (`ConditionContext`) can even be. The context must be an explicitly-passed borrow of a SNAPSHOT of already-materialized world (registry view, resolved environment) — not a live mutable handle — and conditions must be synchronous, side-effect-free reads (no `.await`, no construction), because they run inside the bootstrap task before any bean future exists.
- The two inputs differ in shape: Spring passes a live `AnnotatedTypeMetadata` (runtime reflection over annotations). leaf has no annotation reflection — the triggering annotation's attributes must be MATERIALIZED into const metadata at compile time by `annotation-model`, and the condition reads that const slice. So the 'one reusable Condition parameterized by the annotation's attributes' intent works, but only because the attributes were deliberately frozen into data, not read off a type at runtime.
- Spring's two-phase PARSE_CONFIGURATION vs REGISTER_BEAN is an order in a single-threaded reflective parse over a mutable definition set. Rust's analogue must impose this ordering as explicit DATA (a `Phase` on the descriptor) resolved by the runtime planner — NEVER link/section/constructor order, which the cross-crate doc proves is unspecified and may even be randomized. The order-sensitivity that makes OnBean correct cannot be borrowed from the build system; it must be re-established at runtime.
- Generics cannot be link-registered: a `@Conditional` guard attached to a generic `Foo<T>` has no monomorphized symbol, so a const condition descriptor for it has nowhere to live. Spring's type-erased annotations have no such limit; leaf must hard-error on generic guarded items or demand per-instantiation registration (same limitation as component discovery).
- Spring tolerates the most-common authoring bug (a bean-introspecting Condition that forgets to declare REGISTER phase, silently evaluating too early against a half-built world). leaf CAN do better — infer the phase from the condition's declared class (R3) so the bug is structurally impossible — but only the realizations that model conditions as typed/declared data (not opaque closures) can; an opaque `Fn`-based condition would reproduce Spring's footgun.
- Spring's 'unsatisfied condition = graceful no-bean, surfaced only via a report' depends on a runtime report object and DEBUG logging. leaf's equivalent must thread a `ConditionOutcome{matched, reason}` into a `condition-report` sink WITHOUT making a no-match an error — but Rust's bias toward `Result`/early-failure tempts making a missing dependency loud. The clash: condition-not-met and wiring-broken are DISTINCT, time-separated outcomes (Spring is explicit), and leaf must preserve that separation (drop silently now, fail loudly only if something later actually needs the dropped bean) rather than collapse them into one eager error.
- Feature-as-condition (the rust-native OnClass) collides with Cargo feature semantics: features only OR on and cannot be un-set downstream, and a sibling test/example enabling a leaf feature can flip a condition on for a binary that never asked (feature unification). Conditions modeled on features must therefore be strictly ADDITIVE capability-presence checks; any 'choose backend A xor B' must stay a runtime/profile decision, not a cfg condition — a constraint Spring (with a flat mutable classpath) never faces.

### Interactions
- **condition-family** — Primary consumer and the reason this SPI exists. OnClass/OnMissingClass, OnBean/OnMissingBean/OnSingleCandidate, OnProperty, OnExpression, OnResource, OnWebApplication, OnThreading, etc. are all THIN annotations that emit a `ConditionId` + attribute slice against this SPI — no bespoke logic. The nested-boolean helpers (AnyNestedCondition/AllNestedConditions/NoneNestedConditions analogue) are built on this feature's AND-fold / `CondExpr` combinators. This SPI must declare the `Phase`/'earliest decidable tier' classes that let the family map OnClass→cfg/parse and OnBean→register correctly.
- **annotation-model** — Hard upstream dependency. This SPI's second input — the triggering annotation's merged, distance-resolved attribute metadata read WITHOUT constructing the bean — IS the `annotation-model` const descriptor. The `ConditionEntry`/`CondExpr` that gates an element is emitted ONTO that element's annotation-metadata descriptor. Whatever realization annotation-model picks (compile-time-merged const vs runtime engine) determines how/when conditions read their attributes; co-review required.
- **condition-report** — This SPI produces the `ConditionOutcome{matched, reason}` (and in R3, a structured boolean-tree outcome) that condition-report records as positive/negative matches with human-readable reasons. The design rule (from Spring): authoring a good reason string makes the condition debuggable for free. This SPI must thread outcomes to the report sink as a non-error side channel; report owns the accounting/presentation, this owns producing the verdict + reason.
- **profiles** — `@Profile` IS a `@Conditional` — a canned condition (`Leaf(ON_PROFILE, ...)`) that reads `environment.active_profiles()` and evaluates the profile expression. This SPI provides the predicate substrate; profiles provides the `!`/`&`/`|` grammar and the active/include/group levers it evaluates over. Conceptual economy: profiles are not a parallel engine, just the most ergonomically-named condition preset.
- **environment** — A core input to `ConditionContext`: OnProperty/OnExpression/OnProfile/OnCloudPlatform read the (frozen, read-view) Environment — resolved properties + active profiles — at registration-decision time. This SPI consumes Environment's read-only `PropertyResolver` view synchronously; the Environment must be sealed/built before the condition-evaluation pass runs (a `refresh-lifecycle` ordering constraint).
- **bean-registry / bean-definition** — `ConditionContext` exposes a read-mostly view of the bean-definition registry so OnBean/OnMissingBean/OnSingleCandidate can probe 'what has been defined so far'. This is the order-sensitive input that forces the REGISTER phase: the registry view must be complete (all definitions collected) before register-phase conditions read it. A false condition means the definition is never added to the registry (graceful no-bean).
- **auto-configuration / auto-config-ordering** — The reason the two-phase model matters in practice: auto-config beans are guarded by OnMissingBean and ordered to run AFTER all user config, so they back off silently when the user defines their own. This SPI's register-phase + deterministic-ordering guarantee is what makes that back-off correct. Spring scopes OnBean/OnMissingBean to auto-config classes precisely because that's the only place 'defined so far' is stable; leaf must carry the same scoping discipline.
- **component-scanning / programmatic-registration** — Discovered components and programmatic (BeanRegistrar-analogue) registrations are gated by this SPI before becoming live definitions — scanning's candidates and registrar emissions both pass through condition evaluation. The condition descriptors ride the SAME cross-crate discovery substrate as the components themselves (shared `&[Descriptor]` table), so conditions don't invent a separate channel.
- **expression-language** — OnExpression-style conditions delegate predicate evaluation to the runtime expression layer (`#{...}` analogue). This SPI provides the hook point and the `ConditionContext` the expression evaluates against; expression-language provides the evaluation engine. (Spring notes referencing a bean in such an expression forces very early init — a hazard this SPI's read-mostly/synchronous contract must guard against.)
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Condition evaluation is a fixed step in the bring-up state machine: it runs during definition-loading/registration, after the Environment is sealed and (for register-phase) after all definitions are collected, but before eager singleton instantiation. The parse-vs-register two-pass IS a sub-ordering within refresh; this SPI must slot into refresh-lifecycle's ordered template, not run ad hoc.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- THE conditional strategy (charter §3, explicitly deferred): which tier owns which condition kind — `cfg`/feature gates (compile-time, OnClass-analogue), build/codegen const-folds (build-time, target/config-knowable), or runtime predicates over the descriptor table (OnBean/profiles/runtime-property)? This feature's three realizations ARE candidate points on that axis; Phase 2 must decide the blend, not Phase 1. Directly couples to the metadata/codegen-boundary and compile-vs-runtime-resolution decisions.
- Is the rust-native `OnClass`/`OnMissingClass` realized as a Cargo feature, a `cfg`, a runtime registry probe, or a combination — and how does that reconcile with feature additivity (features only OR on, can't be un-set, and a sibling can flip one on via unification)? This decides whether 'optional-dependency presence' is a build fact or a runtime fact, and ripples into auto-configuration's back-off model.
- How is the two-phase parse-vs-register ORDERING established at runtime when link/section/constructor order is unspecified (and possibly randomized)? This rides entirely on the cross-crate composition & tooling decision (link-time slice vs inventory vs build-codegen) and on a deterministic topo/phase sort over descriptor DATA — shared with auto-config-ordering and component discovery ordering.
- Where exactly does the `ConditionContext`'s read-mostly world-state view come from, and what is its ownership/lifetime shape (a borrowed snapshot of the registry + sealed environment vs an Arc handle)? This is an instance of the deferred scope/lifetime/ownership keystone applied to the condition-evaluation pass, and must agree with how bean-registry and environment expose read views.
- Should the condition `Phase` be author-declared (Spring-faithful, risks the forgot-to-declare-REGISTER bug) or framework-INFERRED from the condition's declared class / expression tree (R3, makes the bug impossible but can surprise)? This is a DX-vs-faithfulness call coupled to whether conditions are opaque objects or typed/declared data.
- Is there ONE shared 'predicate over container state + metadata' primitive that this feature, programmatic-registration's conditional registration, and even collection-injection/candidate-resolver filtering all consume (per unification invariant #11) — or does each carry its own gating? Wants resolution alongside the registry substrate so all gating reads one ConditionContext shape.
- How do condition outcomes thread into the unified error/diagnostics + report model so that condition-not-met (silent no-bean now) and wiring-broken (loud failure later, when something needs the dropped bean) stay DISTINCT and time-separated, while still giving the #9 rich-context account? Connects the deferred unified-diagnostics decision with condition-report.
- Do any condition-relevant inputs require RUNTIME-overlaid state (e.g. a property set by a runtime/dynamic config source, or a dynamic/WASM-module-contributed definition needing gating) that a fully compile-time realization (R2) cannot see — forcing a runtime evaluation layer to always exist? Decides whether the static path can ever be total or is intrinsically a fast-path over a mandatory runtime planner.
- How do conditions interact with the optional dynamic/WASM-module 'second classpath' — a dynamically-discovered definition has no compile-time cfg and no Rust type to gate on, so it can ONLY be gated at runtime; does that make the runtime condition layer mandatory for origin-agnosticism even if static gating is the default for native crates?

### Notes
Scope discipline: I treated `conditions` strictly as the PREDICATE SPI / two-phase model / read-mostly context / AND-composition / graceful-no-bean semantics — the unifying engine. I deliberately did NOT design the rich parameterized condition set (`condition-family`), the match/no-match accounting and presentation (`condition-report`), the metadata view conditions read (`annotation-model`), or the profile grammar (`profiles`); those are distinct catalog neighbours this feature plugs into.

The genuinely divergent axis for THIS feature is WHERE the predicate is evaluated and WHAT a condition is — which is exactly the charter's deferred 'conditional strategy (cfg vs build-time codegen vs runtime gates — likely a blend)'. The three realizations span that axis on purpose: R1 = pure runtime SPI over a descriptor table (most Spring-faithful, most open/origin-agnostic, handles OnBean/profiles, but pays a cold-path cost and can't express OnClass); R2 = compile-time cfg/feature + codegen const-fold (zero overhead, strongest 'read-without-loading' via cfg-removal, earliest diagnostics, but intrinsically PARTIAL — cannot express runtime-state conditions); R3 = a typed `CondExpr` algebra with framework-inferred phase that acts as the SEAM letting a single guard const-fold its cheap leaves and runtime-eval its order-sensitive ones.

Strong recommendation to Phase 2/3: the realizations are COMPOSABLE along a 'how early can this be decided' axis, not mutually exclusive — almost certainly a hybrid. Likely shape: R3's expression/phase-inference algebra as the authoring/composition surface; R2's cfg-gate as the rust-native realization of OnClass/optional-dependency presence (the cleanest 'read without loading'); R1's runtime planner as the mandatory layer for OnBean/profiles/runtime-property and for gating dynamic/WASM-contributed definitions (origin-agnosticism makes a runtime layer non-optional). Two sub-intents of 'read without loading' to keep separate in Phase 2: (a) decide WITHOUT running the bean's constructor (all three do this) vs (b) Spring's literal 'name a class that may be absent without error' — whose true Rust analogue is cfg/feature compilation-gating, NOT a runtime probe (a linked type is always present).

Critical constraints from grounding: the `Condition`/`ConditionContext`/`Phase`/`ConditionOutcome`/`CondExpr` ABI must live in `leaf-core` and stay ultra-stable+minimal (emitted const data hard-codes paths to it workspace-wide). Two-phase ORDERING must be DATA resolved at runtime/codegen, NEVER link/section/constructor order (unspecified, possibly randomized). Condition descriptors ride the SAME cross-crate discovery substrate as components/definitions (don't invent a separate channel) and inherit its DCE + Layer-0 hazards — a startup self-check must assert both the gated definitions AND the ConditionId→implementation registry resolved (empty registries pass/fail silently). Nightly review: nothing here needs nightly — runtime trait objects, const data, cfg/features, const-eval folds, and inventory/linkme are all stable; conditions are synchronous cold-path reads so no async-trait boxing tax. The co-review partner is `condition-family` (first consumer; its OnClass↔cfg and OnBean↔register-phase mapping is the acid test of whichever blend is chosen) and `auto-configuration` (whose back-off correctness depends entirely on the register-phase + deterministic ordering this SPI guarantees).

## Conditional-On-X Family  `[condition-family]`

This feature is the RICH PARAMETERIZED CONDITION SET that sits ON TOP of the `conditions` SPI (the pure-predicate/two-phase/AND-fold/graceful-no-bean engine, already designed upstream as R1 runtime-predicate-table / R2 cfg+codegen-static / R3 typed-CondExpr-algebra). condition-family owns the named, attribute-parameterized members and the nested-boolean helpers — it does NOT own the predicate contract, phase model, ConditionContext shape, report accounting, annotation-metadata view, or the profile grammar (those are conditions/condition-report/annotation-model/profiles).

Spring intent to replicate (NOT mechanism), from the `@ConditionalOnX` family section:
(1) A SET of thin, reusable, attribute-parameterized conditions, each one shell over the SPI: OnClass/OnMissingClass (class-presence, ASM-read so naming an ABSENT optional dependency never throws — the sharp edge: a @Bean method's param/return types load BEFORE the method-level condition, so the class condition must be hoisted onto a nested config); OnBean/OnMissingBean/OnSingleCandidate (order-sensitive, REGISTER phase, default the target type to a @Bean method's return type, accept a SearchStrategy CURRENT/ANCESTORS/ALL over the factory hierarchy; OnSingleCandidate means 'by-type autowire would resolve unambiguously' incl. @Primary — NOT 'exactly one bean'); OnProperty (prefix+name key, matches when present and not 'false' by default, havingValue pins exact case-insensitive value, matchIfMissing controls absent-property behaviour, multiple names must ALL pass) and the boolean-specific OnBooleanProperty (present AND ==true by default); OnExpression (delegates to the expression layer; referencing a bean forces very early init); OnResource; OnWebApplication(SERVLET/REACTIVE)/OnNotWebApplication; OnWarDeployment; OnCloudPlatform; OnJava(Range); plus lesser ones OnThreading (platform vs virtual), OnCheckpointRestore, OnJndi, OnMissingFilterBean; and the container forms OnProperties/OnBooleanProperties.
(2) NESTED-BOOLEAN HELPERS — stacked conditions are pure AND; OR/AND/NOR require the AnyNestedCondition/AllNestedConditions/NoneNestedConditions helpers, each declaring member conditions and choosing the ConfigurationPhase. leaf should make this first-class, not subclass-boilerplate.
(3) Every member produces a ConditionOutcome with a human-readable reason ('did not find property', 'found beans of type X') so condition-report is populated for FREE — writing a good message IS the report.
(4) The two-phase contract MUST be honoured per kind: OnClass-family is PARSE (order-insensitive, prunes cheaply, possibly compile-time); OnBean-family is REGISTER (order-sensitive, scoped to auto-config where 'defined so far' is stable). Family members must declare the right phase/earliest-decidable tier so auto-configuration back-off (OnMissingBean after user config) is correct.
The reframing leaf MUST do: Spring's OnClass-against-absent-class is a runtime ASM probe; Rust has no runtime reflection and a linked type is ALWAYS present, so 'optional-dependency presence' becomes a Cargo-feature/cfg compile-time fact — a different mechanism for the same intent. OnWebApplication/OnThreading/OnCloudPlatform similarly map onto build-cfg or runtime-environment facts, not classpath probes.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Built-in condition CATALOG: each Conditional-On-X is a registered Condition impl + a thin attribute macro emitting (ConditionId, attr-slice) onto the element descriptor
Treat the family as a flat CATALOG of concrete Condition impls shipped by a normal `leaf-conditions` (family) crate, each addressed by a stable `ConditionId`, directly riding the upstream R1 runtime-predicate-table SPI. For every member there is (a) a hand-written `Condition` impl in the family crate — `OnPropertyCondition`, `OnBeanCondition`, `OnFeatureCondition` (the OnClass analogue), `OnExpressionCondition`, `OnResourceCondition`, `OnWebApplicationCondition`, `OnThreadingCondition`, `OnCloudPlatformCondition`, `OnRustVersionCondition` (OnJava analogue), etc. — that reads its OWN typed attribute slice off the triggering element's annotation-model metadata and probes the correct ConditionContext facet (PropertyResolver for OnProperty, registry view for OnBean, a capability set for OnFeature, the sealed Environment for OnWebApplication); and (b) a thin attribute macro per member, e.g. `#[on_property(name="x.enabled", having_value="true", match_if_missing)]`, that emits ONLY a const `ConditionEntry { id: leaf::cond::ON_PROPERTY, attrs: &[Attr::Str("name","x.enabled"), ...] }` appended to the descriptor — zero predicate logic in the macro.
